08  Conversational Maxims and Conversational Implicature
An implicature can result through the flouting of one of the maxims by the speaker (B),
in  which  the  hearer  (A)  can  infer  something  not  explicitly  said  if the  speaker  (B)
disregards  one  of the  maxims  (whether  intentionally  or  not),  though  the  hearer  (A)
assumes that the speaker is not doing so.  Give an implicature of B’s utterance in each
of the  following situations,  and  then  identify the maxim(s)  (i.e.  relevance,  quantity,  or
manner) that has/have been  flouted (and thus which  led the hearer to this  implicature).
Note  that  none  of the  implicatures  from  B’s  utterances  and  actually  entailed  by  the
| sentences  | uttered  by  | B.  |     |

| j Selectional    | and  Subcategorization  |                | Restrictions |
| ---------------- | ----------------------- | -------------- | ------------ |
| 1.  Selectional  | Restrictions            |                |              |
| (1)  !The        | keyboard  designed      | some clothes.  |              |
| (2)  !The        | stapler  took  a        | break.         |              |
| (3)  !My         | colleague  broke        | his  feelings. |              |
Selectional Restrictions refer to the semantic restrictions imposed by predicates on their
arguments.  For example, we might say that the verb design carries a feature [+animate]
and  that  its  subject must also cany  this  feature.  If it doesn’t,  the  resulting sentence  in
deviant. Clearly, in (1) the subject expression the keyboard is not an animate entity and
the sentence is odd as a result. (2) is strange for the same reason. (3) can also be handled
in  terms  of features:  we  might  say  that  the  verb  break carries  the  feature  [+concrete]
which must be  matched  by a direct object that carries  the  same feature.  In (3) the  DO
| is  an  abstract NP,  | and  this  accounts  | for  its  | peculiarity. |
| --------------------- | -------------------- | --------- | ------------ |
2.  Subcategorization
| (4) John  | destroyed  the    | building.       |     |
| --------- | ----------------- | --------------- | --- |
| (5) John  | sent  Mary  some  | money.          |     |
| (6) John  | put  the  book    | on  the  table. |     |
54 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr TV
Subcategorization  Restrictions refer to the syntactic restrictions imposed by predicates
on the number and type of their complements.  Different heads subcategorize for different
complements  and  we  use  so-called  subcategorization frames  to  specify  exactly  which
complements a head takes.  Here’s the subcategorization frame for the verb destroy, send
and put.
| destroy     | (verb)        |     |     |     |
| ----------- | ------------- | --- | --- | --- |
| L,          | NP]           |     |     |     |
| send        | (verb)        |     |     |     |
| L,          | NP  NP]       |     |     |     |
| put         | (verb)        |     |     |     |
| [_,         | NP  PP]       |     |     |     |
| p i         | i i H         |     |     |     |
| i Thematic  | Relations * 1 |     |     |     |
It  has  been  argued that each Argument (i.e.  Subject or Complement)  of a  Predicate
bears a particular thematic role, and that the set of thematic functions which Arguments
can  fulfil  are  drawn  from  a  highly  restricted,  finite,  universal  set.  The  precise  set  of
thematic functions assumed to constitute primitive elements of the associated Theory of
Thematic Structure (or Theta Theory) varies somewhat from author to author:  but in (1)
| below,  | we  list  some  | of the  commonly  | assumed  | theta-roles. |
| ------- | --------------- | ----------------- | -------- | ------------ |
(1) a.  Theme(or  Patient)  =  Entity  undergoing  the  effect  of some  action
|     | {Mary         | fell  over)                    |     |         |
| --- | ------------- | ------------------------------ | --- | ------- |
|     | b.  Agent(or  | Actor)  =  Instigator of some  |     | action  |
|     | {John         | killed  Harry)                 |     |         |
c.  Experience!' =  Entity  experiencing  some  psychological  state
|     | {John  | was  happy) |     |     |
| --- | ------ | ----------- | --- | --- |
Chapter 3 English Syntax • 55

What English Language Teachers Should Know
d. Benefactive = Entity benefiting from some action
(John bought some flowers for Mary)
e. Instrument = Means by which something comes about
(John wounded Harry with a knife)
f. Locative = Place in which something is situated or takes place
(John hid the letter under the bed)
g. Goal = Entity towards which something moves
(John passed the book to Mary)
h. Source = Entity from which something moves
(John returned front Paris)
A number of linguists have argued that the thematic structure of a sentence plays a
much more pervasive role in Syntax. For example, it has been argued that the distribution
of certain types of Adverbial and Prepositional phrases is thematically determined: thus,
Gruber argues that Adverbs like deliberately can co-occur only with Agent phrases: cf.
(2) a. John (=Agent) deliberately rolled the ball down the hill
b. *T/te ball (=Theme) deliberately rolled down the hill
Likewise, Fillmore argues that the Adverb personally can only co-occur with
Experiencer arguments: cf.
(3) a. Personally, / (=Experiencer) don’t like roses
b. Personally, your proposal doesn’t interest me (=Experiencer)
c. * Personally, / (=Agent) hit you
d. * Personally, you hit me (=Theme)
And in much the same vein, Anderson argues that only Verbs with Agent subjects
permit by-phrase nominal counterparts:
56 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr
|     | (4) a.  The  | mayor    | (=Agent)  | protested  |       |     |
| --- | ------------ | -------- | --------- | ---------- | ----- | --- |
|     | b.  the      | protest  | by  the   | mayor      |       |     |
|     | (5) a.  The  | mayor    | (=Theme)  |            | died  |     |
|     | b.  *the     | death    | by  the   | mayor      |       |     |
Many  other  areas  of  Syntax  have  also  been  argued  to  be  subject  to  thematic
constraints.  For example,  Fillmore argues that only constituents with the same Thematic
Function  can  be  conjoined.  So,  for  example,  since  the  two  subject  NPs  John  and  a
hammer  play  different  thematic  roles  in  the  following  sentences:
|     | (6) a.  John  | (=Agent)   | broke         | the  | window |             |
| --- | ------------- | ---------- | ------------- | ---- | ------ | ----------- |
|     | b.            | A  hammer  | (instrument)  |      | broke  | the  window |
the  two  subject  NPs  cannot  be  idiomatically  conjoined  -  hence  the  oddity  of:
|             | (7) 11 John  | and        | a  hammer  | broke  | the          | window    |
| ----------- | ------------ | ---------- | ---------- | ------ | ------------ | --------- |
| Structural  |              | Ambiguity  |            | and    | Constituent  | Structure |
The  sentence  The  boy  saw  the  man  with  a  telescope  is  an  instance  of structural
ambiguity.  It  is  ambiguous  because  it  can  have  more  than  one  meaning.
Meaning  1:  The  boy  saw  the  man  who  was  holding  a  telescope.
|     | Meaning  | 2:  The  | boy  saw  | the  | man  | by  using  a  telescope. |
| --- | -------- | -------- | --------- | ---- | ---- | ------------------------ |
The  sentence  is  structurally  ambiguous  because  it  is  associated  with  two  different
phrase structures, each corresponding to a different meaning.  Here are the two structures:

3. Coordination
(20) Frank washed and ironed his shirts yesterday, (coordinated main verbs)
(21) Frank washed his shirts and polished his shoes yesterday, (coordinated lower
V-bars)
(22) Frank washed his shirts yesterday and last week, (coordinated adjunct NPs)
(23) Frank washed his shirts yesterday and polished his shoes last week (coordinated
higher V-bars)
(24) Julius loudly announced and decried the election victory, (coordinated main verbs)
(25) Julius loudly announced the election victory and gave an interview to the press.
(coordinated lower V-bars)
(26) Julius loudly and disgustedly announced the election victory, (coordinated AdvPs)
(27) Julius loudly announced the election victory and disgustedly gave an interview
to the press, (coordinated higher V-bars)
Only the same kind of constituents can be coordinated.
U. Cleft and Pseudo-cleft Sentences
(1) Cleft Sentences
(28) It was Frank who washed his shirts yesterday.
(29) It was his shirts that Frank washed yesterday.
(30) It was yesterday that Frank washed his shirts.
(2) Pseudo-cleft Sentences
(31) What Frank washed yesterday was his shirts.
(32) What Frank did yesterday was wash his shirts.
(33) What Frank did was wash his shirts yesterday.
Only constituents can occur in the focus position.

|                  |     |     | id   | Adjuncts  |     |         |     |
| ---------------- | --- | --- | ---- | --------- | --- | ------- | --- |
| 1.  Complements  |     |     | and  | Adjuncts  |     | in  VPs |     |
(D  Obligatoriness
Complements are strictly-required phrases whereas adjuncts are not. The examples in
(l)-(3) show that the verb placed requires an  NP and a  PP as  its complements, kept an
| NP  and  | a  PP  | or an  | AP,  and  | stayed  | a  PP. |     |     |
| -------- | ------ | ------ | --------- | ------- | ------ | --- | --- |
Chapter 3 English Syntax • 67

What English Language Teachers Should Know
(1) a. John placed Kim behind the garage.
b. John kept him behind the garage.
c. *John stayed Kim behind the garage.
(2) a. *John placed him busy.
b. John kept him busy.
c. *John stayed him busy.
(3) a. *John placed behind the counter.
b. *John kept behind the counter.
c. John stayed behind the counter.
In contrast, adjuncts are optional. Their presence is not required by grammar:
(4) a. John deposited some money in the bank.
b. John deposited some money in the bank on Friday.
In (4b), the PP on Friday is optional here, serving as a adjunct.
(2) Iterability
The possibility of iterating identical types of phrase can also distinguish between
complements and adjuncts. In general two or more instances of the same adjunct type
can occur with the same head, but this is impossible for complements.
(5) a. *The UN blamed global warming [on humans] [on natural causes].
b. Kim and Sandy met [in Seoul] [in the lobby of the Lotte Hotel] in March.
In (5a) on humans is a complement and thus the same type of PP on natural causes
cannot co-occur. Yet in Seoul is an adjunct and we can repeatedly have the same type
of PP.
(3) Do-so test
Another reliable test often used to distinguish complements from adjuncts is the do
so test. As shown in (6), we can use do so to avoid repetition of an identical VP
expression:
68 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr^^
(6) a. John deposited some money in the checking account and Mary did so (too).
b. John deposited some money in the checking account on Friday and Mary did
so (too).
What we can observe in (6b) is that the VP did so can replace either the minimal
phrase deposited some money in the checking account or the maximal phrase including
the adjunct on Friday. Notice that this VP can replace only the minimal phrase, leaving
out the adjunct.
(7) John deposited some money in the checking account on Friday and Mary did
so on Monday.
From these observations, we can draw the conclusion that if something can be replaced
by do so, then it is either a minimal or a maximal phrase. This in turn means that this
‘replacement’ VP cannot be understood to leave out any complement(s). This can be
verified with more data:
(8) a. *John [deposited some money in the checking account] and Mary did so
in the saving account.
b. *John [gave a present to the student] and Mary did so to the teacher.
Here the PPs in the checking account and to the student are both complements, and
thus they should be included in the do so phrase. This gives us the following informal
generalization:
(9) Do-so Replacement Condition:
The phrase do so can replace a verb phrase which includes at least all the
complements of the verb.
This condition explains why we cannot have another locative complement phrase in
the savings account or to the teacher in (8). The unacceptability of the examples in (10)
also supports this generalization about English grammar:
Chapter 3 English Syntax • 69

ilM kl
What English Language Teachers Should Know
(10) a.  *John  locked  Fido  in  the  garage  and  Mary  did  so  in  the  room,
|      | b.  *John  | ate        | a  carrot  | and  Mary  did  | so  a  radish. |
| ---- | ---------- | ---------- | ---------- | --------------- | -------------- |
| (4)  | Ordering   | difference |            |                 |                |
Another difference that follows from the structural  distinction between  complements
and adjuncts is an ordering difference. As a complement needs to combine with a lexical
| head  | first,  adjuncts  | follow  | complements:  |                      |     |
| ----- | ----------------- | ------- | ------------- | -------------------- | --- |
|       | (11) a.  John     | met     | [a  student]  | [in  the  park],     |     |
|       | b.  *John         | met     | [in  the      | park]  [a  student]. |     |
Adverb  Phrases express  information about time,  manner,  reason, possibility,  speaker
intention,  and  so  on.  The occur  in  a  wide  range  of positions  and  can  be  inserted  into
almost any sentence, to add more information and detail. Adverb phrases are thus typically
adjuncts rather than complements; verbs and other heads do not typically select adverbs.
Rather,  adverb  phrases  can  occur  fairly  indiscriminately  in  any  sentence.
There are a  few cases  in which adverb phrases appear to be selected  by verbs.  One
| is  | given  in  (12). |                   |     |                       |     |
| --- | ---------------- | ----------------- | --- | --------------------- | --- |
|     | (12) The         | official  worded  |     | the  bill  carefully. |     |
Typically,  we  don’t just  word something,  we  word something  in a particular  way.
Consequently,  (12)  sounds  oddly  incomplete  if we  omit  the  adverb  phrase  carefully.
| 2.  | Complements          | and  | Adjuncts  | in  NPs |     |
| --- | -------------------- | ---- | --------- | ------- | --- |
| (D  | Ordering  difference |      |           |         |     |
In  the  tree  structure,  one  can  find  out  the  difference  between  the  complement  and
the  adjunct by  looking at which  element  is  sister to  which  one.  For  instance,  in (1) of
physics  is sister to N  and  is therefore the complement,  whereas from England is not a
sister to  N  and  is  therefore  the adjunct.  There can  only  be  one complement but  many
adjuncts and the order between complement and adjunct cannot be reversed as (2) shows.
This is the  first argument you can use to distinguish between complements and adjuncts:
70 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr "
(1)
(2) *A teacher from England of physics.
(2) One-replacement test
Apart from word order, there is a second way to distinguish complements from
adjuncts and it involves determining what pronoun one can use to pronominalize certain
parts of the NP. In (1), teacher of physics and teacher of physics from England are N's.
There is an argument that the status of N' is different from that of N and NP. It can
be shown that the N1 can be replaced by one, but that the N cannot be replaced by one.
In (3), one replaces teacher of physics, i.e. an N1, and the sentence is grammatical; in
(4), one replaces teacher, i.e. an N, and this results in an ungrammatical sentence:
(3) I know the teacher of physics from England and the one from France.
(4) *1 know the teacher of physics from England and the one of chemistry.
Hence, the first piece of evidence for distinguishing complement and adjunct is that
the complement is closer to the head, as in (1). Secondly, there is also evidence for the
special status of the intermediate category N' in that it can be replaced by one.
(3) Coordination test
A third test for distinguishing complements from adjuncts is coordination. It is possible
to coordinate two complements, as in (5), or two adjuncts, as in (6), but not a complement
and an adjunct, as in (7):
Chapter 3 English Syntax • 71

What English Language Teachers Should Know
(5) The teachers of physics and of chemistry.
(6) The teachers from Turkey and from Spain.
(7) *The teachers from Turkey and of physics.
Verb Complementation: V + NP + to-infinitive
J
1. V + [NP + to-infinitive]
(D Meaning
(1) Ed believes [the story to be false]
(2) Ed believes the story.
(3) Ed believes that the story is false.
What Ed believes is the proposition that the story is false
The predicate believe takes only one internal argument.
(2) Dummy Elements and Idiom Chunks
(4) Ed believes it always to be raining in London.
(5) Ed believes there to be a traitor in the company.
Expletives that occur only in the subject position occupy the postverbal position.
(6) Ed believes the coast to be clear.
(7) Ed believes the fat to be in the fire.
Subject expressions occur in the postverbal position.
(3) Postverbal Passivisation
(8) Ed believes [the jury to have given the wrong verdict].
(9) Ed believes [the wrong verdict to have been given by the jury].

3. V + [NP + to-infinitive]
(1) Meaning
(17) Kate wants Ralph to get out of her life.
What Kate wants is the proposition that Ralph gets out of her life.
The predicate want takes only one internal argument.
(2) Dummy Elements and Idiom Chunks
(18) Kate wanted it to rain on Ralph’s birthday.
(19) Ralph wanted there to be a ceasefire between him and Kate.
Expletives occupy the postverbal position.
(20) Kate wants the coast to be clear, in order for her to escape form Ralph.
(21) Kate doesn’t want the fat to be in the fire, because of some stupid action of
Ralph’s.
Subject expressions occur in the postverbal position.
(3) Postverbal Passivisation
(22) Kate wanted Janet to poison Ralph.
(23) Kate wanted Ralph to be poisoned by Janet.
Postverbal passivization does not cause a change in meaning.
U. Summary
(24) Ed believes the story to be false.
(23) Ed persuaded Brian to interview Melanie.
(28) Kate wants Ralph to get out of her life.
1U • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr
All  three  sentences  conform  to  the  pattern  verb  +  NP +  to-infinitive,  and  yet  they
are syntactically different:  in the believe + NP + to-infinitive construction the postverbal
NP  functions  as the  Subject  of a  subordinate  clause,  while  the NP  in  thep ersuade  +
NP  + to-infinitive  construction  functions  as a  Direct  Object. In  the want  +  NP  +
to-infinitive construction  the  postverbal  NP  is  also  the  Subject of a  subordinate clause,
but, unlike the postverbal NP in  the believe + NP + to-infinitive construction,  it cannot
| be  | fronted  when  | the  | matrix  clause  | is  passivised. |     |     |     |

fronted  under passivisation.

CHECKPOINT
Control and Raising
We will discuss two classes of predicates in English. Members of the first class,
including verbs and adjective like persuade, order, force, forbid, urge, try, plan, pretend,
intend, refuse, agree, hope, eager, reluctant, are often referred to as CONTROL
PREDICATES. Members of the second class, verbs and adjectives like believe, consider,
expect, imagine, seem, appear, likely, certain, happen, tend, begin, etc., are often
referred to as RAISING PREDICATES.
Predicates in both classes take an infinitival open complement, whose subject is
controlled by an element of the matrix clause. However, there are several systematic
differences between the two classes which require an explanation. We will begin by listing
some of these differences, then attempt to develop an analysis which will account for
the data. We will suggest that the two classes of predicates are essentially identical in
terms of assignment of grammatical relations and phrase structure configuration. The
crucial difference between the two concerns the assignment of semantic roles.
1. Dummy subjects
Certain verbs in English allow the pro-forms it or there to appear as “dummy”
subjects. This means that the pro-form appears in the subject position and bears the subject
relation, but has no semantic content; it does not take an antecedent (in the usual sense)
and is not assigned any semantic role by the verb. Predicates of the Raising type (1) allow
dummy subjects to appear in their subject position, whereas predicates of the Control type
(2) do not: 1
(1) a. It seems to be raining.
b. There are likely to be a lot of accidents if we get a snow-storm.
c. It tends to get dark in the valley by mid-afternoon.
d. There began to emerge a pattern in the criminal’s behavior.
Chapter 3 English Syntax • 79

What English Language Teachers Should Know
| (2)  a.  | *It  tries  | to  be  | raining. |     |     |
| -------- | ----------- | ------- | -------- | --- | --- |
b.  *There  are  eager to  be  a  lot  of accidents  if we  get  a  snow-storm.
| c.  *It  | intends  | to  get  | dark  | in  the  | valley  by  mid-afternoon. |
| -------- | -------- | -------- | ----- | -------- | -------------------------- |
d.  *There  refused  to  emerge a  pattern  in  the  criminal’s  behavior.
| 2.  Selectional  | restrictions |     |     |     |     |
| ---------------- | ------------ | --- | --- | --- | --- |
Predicates  of the  Control  type  may  impose selectional  restrictions on  their subjects.
This  is  illustrated  by  the  unacceptability of (3b)  and  (4b).  However,  predicates  of the
Raising type do not impose selectional  restrictions on their subjects, as illustrated in (3a)
and  (4a):
| (3) a.  That  | brick  | appears  | to  be  | cracked,  |     |
| ------------- | ------ | -------- | ------- | --------- | --- |
| b.  #That     | brick  | tried    | to  be  | cracked.  |     |
(4) a.  Your  radio  is  likely  to  be  confiscated  by  the  customs  officers,
b.  #Your  radio  is  eager to  be  confiscated  by  the  customs  officers.
3.  Passivization
When the complement of a predicate of the Control  type  is passivized, the meaning
of the sentence as a whole is altered. This is illustrated in (6) and (8).  But with predicates
of  the  Raising  type,  passivization  does  not  affect  the  propositional  meaning  in  any
significant  way.  That  is,  either  form  could  be used  to  describe the  same  situation,  and
if one sentence  is  true the other must be true as well.  This  is  illustrated  in (5) and (7):
| (5) a.  Dr.  | Benson      | is  likely        | to            | examine   | John.            |
| ------------ | ----------- | ----------------- | ------------- | --------- | ---------------- |
| b.  John     | is          | likely  to        | be  examined  |           | by  Dr.  Benson. |
| (no          | difference  | in                | meaning)      |           |                  |
| (6) a.  Dr.  | Benson      | is  reluctant to  |               | examine   | John.            |
| b.  John     | is          | reluctant to      | be            | examined  | by  Dr.  Benson. |
| (clear       | difference  |                   | in  meaning)  |           |                  |
80 ® What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr^wc'
(7) a. All the girls seem to like John.
b. John seems to be liked by all the girls,
(no difference in meaning)
(8) a. All the girls try to like John.
b. John tries to be liked by all the girls,
(clear difference in meaning)
U. Idiom chunks
Idiomatic phrases consist of several words, but the meaning of the phrase is not
predictable from the meanings of the individual words. The entire phrase functions
semantically as a single lexical unit. Predicates of the Raising type may allow one part
(or “chunk ”) of an idiomatic expression to appear in the subject position, without losing
the idiomatic meaning of the expression as a whole. This is illustrated in the (a) sentences
in (9-12). With predicates of the Control type, however, this is never possible, as
illustrated in the corresponding (b) sentences.
(9) a. Tabs continue to be kept on the leading suspects,
b. #Tabs intend to be kept on the leading suspects.
{keep tubs on = ‘to watch or monitor someone’s movements’)
(10) a. John’s goose seems to be cooked,
b. #John’s goose tries to be cooked.
(his goose is cooked = ‘lie is in serious trouble )
(11) a. The cat appears to be out of the bag.
b. #The cat refuses to be out of the bag.
{to let the cat out of the bag = ‘to reveal a secret )
(12) a. John’s name is certain to be mud if anyone finds out about this.
b. #John’s name is reluctant to be mud if anyone finds out about this.
{his name is mad = ‘lie is disgraced/unpopular’)

What English Language Teachers Should Know

|     |              | Phenomena  |     | of Reflexives and  |     |          | Pronouns |
| --- | ------------ | ---------- | --- | ------------------ | --- | -------- | -------- |
| 1.  | Antecedents  |            | of  | Reflexive          |     | Pronouns |          |
(1) Agreement
Pronominal  forms  always  reflect  some  of the  inherent  semantic  features  of  their
antecedent.  In English, all pronouns must agree with their antecedents in person, number,
and gender. Thus the masculine subject in (la) requires a masculine reflexive, the feminine
subject in  (lb) requires a feminine reflexive, and  the third  person  plural  subject in (lc)
| requires  | a       | third  person  |                 | plural   | reflexive.                    |     |     |
| --------- | ------- | -------------- | --------------- | -------- | ----------------------------- | --- | --- |
|           | (1) a.  | My             | brother         | admires  | himself/*herself.             |     |     |
|           |         | b.  My         | sister admires  |          | *himself/herself.             |     |     |
|           |         | c.  My         | parents         | admire   | *himself7*herself/themselves. |     |     |
(2)  Domain
The examples in (1) not only show that reflexives must agree with their antecedents,
they illustrate something else as well: reflexive pronouns must find an eligible antecedent
within  their  immediate  sentence.  The  form  herself would  be  ungrammatical  in  (la)
because  that  sentence  does  not  contain  a  possible  antecedent  (i.e.,  a  feminine  singular
NP).  Even  if a potential  antecedent  is  available  in  the  immediately preceding sentence,
as  in  the  example  below,  the  form  herself would  still  be  ungrammatical:
(la') We saw  Mrs. Thatcher on television  last night.  *My brother admires herself
greatly.
Such examples show that a rellexive pronoun may not be separated from its antecedent
by a sentence boundary.  In  fact, even an  internal  clause boundary may not separate the
| two,  | as  demonstrated  |     | by  | the  | examples  | in  (2): |     |
| ----- | ----------------- | --- | --- | ---- | --------- | -------- | --- |
(2) a.  *My  uncle  is  not generous,  but  [I  love  himself anyway].
b.  Mary  suspects  that  [John  admires  himself/*herself too  much].
|     |     | c.  Mary  | waited  | for  | [John  | to  excuse  himself/*herselfj. |     |
| --- | --- | --------- | ------- | ---- | ------ | ------------------------------ | --- |
d.  I  told  you  that  [Mary  would  blame  *myself7*yourself /herself].
82 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr^-.:'
In other words, a reflexive and its antecedent must be elements of the same simple
clause; the smallest clause which contains either one of them must also contain the other.
For this reason, we say that the relationship between a reflexive and its antecedent in
English in clause-bounded.
2. Antecedents of Regular Pronouns
Non-reflexive pronouns must also agree with their antecedents in person, number, and
gender. However, unlike reflexives, “regular” pronouns cannot take an antecedent within
their same minimal clause. The examples in (3) and (4) show that the antecedent of a
regular pronoun cannot be the subject or object of the same simple sentence. (The “#”
sign is used here to indicate that a pronoun cannot refer to the same person as a preceding
NP, as explained in the notes following each sentence.)
(3) a. My brother admires #him. {him must refer to someone else)
b. My sister admires #her. {her must refer to someone else)
c. My parents admire #them. {them must refer to someone else)
(4) a. John described Mary to #him/#her.
(pronoun cannot refer to John or Mary)
b. John wrote Mary a poem about #him/#her.
(pronoun cannot refer to John or Mary)
As the examples in (5) demonstrate, regular pronouns may take an antecedent within
the same sentence provided it is not part of the same minimal clause. In other words,
there must be a clause boundary between the pronoun and its antecedent.
(5) a. My uncle is not generous, but [I love him anyway].
{him can refer to ‘my uncle’)
b. Mary suspects that [John admires her/#him too much].
{her can refer to Mary, but him cannot refer to John)
c. Mary waited for [John to excuse #him/her],
{her can refer to Mary, but him cannot refer to John)

What English Language Teachers Should Know
|     | d.  I  | told  you        | that  | [Mary  | would  | be  blame  | me/you/#her].  |
| --- | ------ | ---------------- | ----- | ------ | ------ | ---------- | -------------- |
|     | (her   | eannot refer to  |       |        | Mary)  |            |                |
When we compare these examples with the parallel reflexive examples  in (1-2), we
see that reflexive and non-reflexive pronouns appear to be in complementary distribution:
if the  reflexive  form  is  grammatical, then  the corresponding  regular pronoun could  not
| be  | used  to  refer to  |     | the  same  | antecedent,  |     | and  vice  | versa. |

Complementizers
| 1.  | Appositive  | that-clauses  |     |     | and  | relative  | clauses |
| --- | ----------- | ------------- | --- | --- | ---- | --------- | ------- |
The  use  of that as  both  a  relative  pronoun  and  a  pure  complementizer can  lead  to
| confusion  | between  | appositive  |     | that-clauses  |        | and  relative         | clauses: |
| ---------- | -------- | ----------- | --- | ------------- | ------ | --------------------- | -------- |
|            | (1) The  | suggestion  |     | that  he      | might  | fail  is  disturbing. |          |
|            | (2) The  | suggestion  |     | that  he      | made   | is  disturbing.       |          |
84 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

lllll
www.amoriteaching.co.kr^vr
the suggestion that he made
In (1) is a clause functioning as a complement of N (equivalent to a direct object
of the verb suggest: Someone suggested that he might fail). The complementizer that
has no function in its own clause, and the embedded clause is complete without that
and can stand alone as a complete sentence He might fail. The //raZ-clause can also
function as an NP: That he might fail is disturbing. In (2) is a clause functioning as
a modifier of N. The relative that has the function of direct object in its own clause,
and hence the embedded clause is not complete without the relative: *He made. The
embedded clause cannot function as an NP: * That he made is disturbing. Note that which
can substitute for that in this case but not in the other: The suggestion which he made
is disturbing. *The suggestion which he might fail is disturbing.
Chapter 3 English Syntax • 85

What English Language Teachers Should Know
2. English complementizers: whether, if
The item whieh might function as a complementizer in English is interrogative if
However, it might seem as if there is a potential parallelism between /'/'and interrogative
wh-adverbs like when/whereAvhether, since they appear to occupy the same position in
sentences like:
(3) 1 don’t know [where/when/whether/if he will go]
Hence we might be tempted to analyse if as an interrogative adverb.
However, there are a number of reasons for rejecting this possibility. For one thing,
//'differs from interrogative adverbs like where/when/whether not only in its form (it isn’t
a >v//-word, as we can see from the fact that it doesn’t begin with wit), but also in the
range of syntactic positions it can occupy. For example, whereas typical wh-adverbs can
occur in finite and infinitive clauses alike, the complementizer if is restricted to
introducing finite clauses:
(4) 1 wonder [where/when/whether/if I should go] [=finite clause]
(5) 1 wonder [where/when/whether/*i f to go] [^infinite clause]
Moreover, if is different from interrogative wh-adverbs in that it cannot be used to
introduce a clause which serves as the complement of a preposition:
(6) I’m not certain about [whether/when/where he’ll go]
(7) *l’m concerned over [/'/'taxes are going to be increased]
(8) *I’m puzzled at [that he should have resigned]
(9) *l’m not very keen on [for you to go there]
Finally, whereas a wh-adverb can typically be coordinated with (e.g. joined by a
coordinating conjunction like and/or) the adverb not, this is not true of if
(10) 1 don’t know [whether or not he’ll turn up]
(11) *1 don’t know [//'or not he’ll turn up]
86 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr^^c
For reasons such as these, it seems more appropriate to categorise if as an  interrogative
complementizer,  and  whether/where/when  as  interrogative  adverbs.
| 3.  | English  | complementizers:  |     |     | whether,  |     | if,  | that,  for |
| --- | -------- | ----------------- | --- | --- | --------- | --- | ---- | ---------- |
Wh-complements can be either tensed or infinitival, and are typically selected by verbs
such as wonder, know, and ask.  Because of their interrogative form, they are sometimes
referred to as indirect questions.  Some examples of such complements are given in (1).
|     | (1) a.  | I  wonder[why  |     | Garth  | Brooks  | likes  | singing  | at  the  fair]. |
| --- | ------- | -------------- | --- | ------ | ------- | ------ | -------- | --------------- |
S
|     | b.  | The  fans  | asked  | [which  | star  | would  | be  singing  | first]. |
| --- | --- | ---------- | ------ | ------- | ----- | ------ | ------------ | ------- |
S
|     | c.  | Trisha  | Yearwood  | doesn’t  | know  | [which  | song  | to  sing], |
| --- | --- | ------- | --------- | -------- | ----- | ------- | ----- | ---------- |
S
|     | d.  | Garth  | Brooks  | decided  | [where  | to  | put  his  | guitar]. |
| --- | --- | ------ | ------- | -------- | ------- | --- | --------- | -------- |
S
The clausal complements  in  (la-b) are tensed, and the ones  in  (lc-d) are  infinitival.
There do exist two possible WH-complementizers, namely whether and if, that occur
| in  | WH-complements,  |     | as  in  | (2). |     |     |     |     |
| --- | ---------------- | --- | ------- | ---- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
(2)  I  wonder  [if/whether  Garth  Brooks  likes  singing  at  the  fair].
S
Whether and if in (2) do not appear in their positions as a result of WFI-Movement;
there is no obvious position in the clause they could have originated  in and been moved
from.  Rather,  they  appear to  originate  in  “complementizer position,”  just  like  that and
for.  Because  we  interpret  them  as  WFI-words,  we  consider  the  clauses  they  introduce
WH-complements,  distinct  from  other  types  of tensed  or  infinitival  complements.  This
distinction  is  further  supported  by  evidence  that  neither  whether  nor  if can  introduce
tensed  or infinitival  complements of verbs that do not select  indirect questions, such as
think and prefer.
Chapter 3 English Syntax • 87

What English Language Teachers Should Know
(3) a.  *1  think  [whether Garth  Brooks  likes  singing  at  the  fair].
S
| b.  *1  prefer  | [if Garth  |     | Brooks  | likes  singing  | at  | the  fair]. |
| --------------- | ---------- | --- | ------- | --------------- | --- | ----------- |
S
Similarly, neither that nor for can introduce indirect questions, as we see in (4), where
these complementizers are ungrammatical in complements selected by wotuler, a verb that
| always  selects  a  WH-complement. |        |        |         |                |     |                 |
| ---------------------------------- | ------ | ------ | ------- | -------------- | --- | --------------- |
| (4) a.  *1  wonder                 | [that  | Garth  | Brooks  | like  singing  |     | at  the  fair]. |
S
| b.  *1  wonder  | [for Trisha  |     | to  sing  | an  encore]. |     |     |
| --------------- | ------------ | --- | --------- | ------------ | --- | --- |
S
We can see from the above discussion and examples that verbs clearly select not only
the category of their complement, namely S, but also the complementizer that introduces
it.
Finally, observe that whether and if are distinct from each  other with  respect to the
kinds  of WH-complements  they  introduce.  As  we  see  in  (5),  whether  introduces  both
| tensed  and  infinitival        | WH-complements. |     |        |     |     |     |
| ------------------------------- | --------------- | --- | ------ | --- | --- | --- |
| (5) a.  I  wonder whether John  |                 |     | left.  |     |     |     |
| b.  I  wonder whether to        |                 |     | leave. |     |     |     |
The sentences in (6) show, in contrast, that //introduces only tensed WH-complements.
| (6) a.  I  wonder  | if John    | left. |            |     |     |     |
| ------------------ | ---------- | ----- | ---------- | --- | --- | --- |
| b.  *1  wonder     | if (John)  |       | to  leave. |     |     |     |
Another interesting point about WH-complementizers is that they are not optional, as
we see by  the ungrammaticality of the sentences in  (7-8).  Whether and if thus contrast
| with  the  optional  English  | complementizers that and for. |     |     |     |     |     |
| ----------------------------- | ----------------------------- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
(7) a.  I  wonder whether Garth  Brooks  likes  singing  at  the  fair.
| b.  *1  wonder Garth  |     | Brooks  | like  | singing  | at  the  | fair. |
| --------------------- | --- | ------- | ----- | -------- | -------- | ----- |
88 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr
|     | (8)  a.  I  wonder  if John  | left,  |     |     |     |
| --- | ---------------------------- | ------ | --- | --- | --- |
|     | b.  *1  wonder John          | left.  |     |     |     |
The above data suggest that English complementizers that, for if,  and  whether have
very distinct properties regarding the types of clausal  complements they  introduce.  That
introduces  tensed,  non-WH-complements,  and  for,  infinitival,  non-WH-complements.
Whether and if on the other hand, introduce only WM-complements; furthermore, whether
introduces  either  tensed  or  infinitival  WH-complements,  but  if  only  tensed
WH-complements.  Finally,  both  that and for are  optional  in  English,  but  whether  and
| if are  | obligatory  in  indirect  | questions  | without  WH-movement. |     |     |
| ------- | ------------------------- | ---------- | --------------------- | --- | --- |
Verbs  therefore  appear  to  select  the  clauses  introduced  by  certain  complementizers.
The  distribution  of complementizers  in  English  is  represented  in  table  below.
|                    |             | +WH                     |     |     | -WH             |
| ------------------ | ----------- | ----------------------- | --- | --- | --------------- |
| Tensed complements |             | whether/if (obligatory) |     |     | that (optional) |
| Infinitival        | complements | whether (obligatory)    |     |     | for (optional)  |

| Case  | Theory |     |     |     |     |

| (1)  Case-Filter  | and  Adjacency  | Requirement |     |     |     |
| ----------------- | --------------- | ----------- | --- | --- | --- |
It is well known that subject NPs of finite clauses get Nominative Case, and the NPs
that are  the complements of a preposition or a verb  bear Accusative Case.  In  addition,
the  NP  that  is  the  subject  of  /^-infinitive  clause  introduced  by  a  prepositional
complementizer for  carries  Accusative  Case.  The  sentences  in  (1)  show  Case-related
phenomena.1
|     | (1) a.  He  often  helps  | her.      |               |           |       |
| --- | ------------------------- | --------- | ------------- | --------- | ----- |
|     | b.  It’s  surprising      | for them  | to  talk  to  | me  like  | that. |
Chapter 3 English Syntax * 89

What English Language Teachers Should Know
In  fact, the case requirement on NPs  is obligatory  in that when an NP fails to have
Case, it results in ungrammaticality. In generative grammar, this  is accounted for by the
| Case  | Filter  and  | Adjacency  | Requirement: |     |     |
| ----- | ------------ | ---------- | ------------ | --- | --- |
Case Filter
Every  overt  NP  must  be  assigned  Case  by  such  appropriate  Case  assignors  as  a
preposition,  the  finite  tense,  complementizer for,  and  a  transitive  verb.
Adjacency Requirement
For  the  Case  assignor  to  assign  Case  to  NPs,  the  two  must  be  adjacent.
|     | Consider  the         | following  | examples:      |                |                     |
| --- | --------------------- | ---------- | -------------- | -------------- | ------------------- |
|     | (2) a.  Poirot        | speaks     | [np  English]  | fluently.      |                     |
|     | b.  *Poirot           | speaks     | fluently       | [np  English]. |                     |
|     | c.  Poirot sincerely  |            | believes       | English        | to  be  important]. |
[ip
|     | d.  *Poirot  | believes  | sincerely  | [ip  English        | to  be  important]. |
| --- | ------------ | --------- | ---------- | ------------------- | ------------------- |
|     | e.  Poirot   | believes  | sincerely  | [cp  that  English  | is  important].     |
In (2a) the verb speak takes an NP complement English and  VP further includes an
adjunct fluently.  The  NP  Poirot  is  case-marked  by  the  finite  INFL;  the NP English  is
case-marked by the transitive verb.  In (2b) the constituents of the sentence are not altered
and yet the sentence  is ungrammatical. The only contrast with  (2a)  is that the V speak
and  the  complement  NP English  are  no  longer  next  to  each  other or adjacent.
A  similar  pattern  is  found  in  (2c)  and  (2d).  In  both  sentences  believe  takes  an  IP
complement.  In  (2c)  the  verb  believe  case-marks  the  subject  NP  of the  lower  clause
(.English) and the sentence  is grammatical, while  in  (2d) the non-adjacency of the verb
and  the  NP to  which  it  should  assign  structural  case  leads  to  ungrammaticality.
The adjacency requirement has nothing to say about (2e).  On the one hand, a finite
clause does  not  need  to  be case-marked. The case  filter applies to NPs, not to clauses.
90 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr
On the other hand, the subject of the  finite clause, the NP English,  will satisfy the case
| filter  | because       | it                 | receives  nominative  |           | from    | the         | finite  I. |
| ------- | ------------- | ------------------ | --------------------- | --------- | ------- | ----------- | ---------- |
|         | Consider the  |                    | following             | examples: |         |             |            |
|         | (3) a.        | I  wonder whether  |                       | [n>       | ec  to  | invite      | Thelma].   |
|         | b.            | *1  wonder         | whether               |           | I/me    | to  invite  | Thelma].   |
[n>
|     | c.  | wonder  | whether  |     | Louise  | to  invite  | Thelma]. |
| --- | --- | ------- | -------- | --- | ------- | ----------- | -------- |
|     |     | *1      |          |     | [n>     |             |          |
In  (3b)  and  (3c)  the  subject  of the  non-finite  clause  is  overt.  The  sentences  are
ungrammatical  because  the  subject  NP  cannot  be  assigned  case.  Whether  is  a
complementizer, but unlike for,  it is not a preposition, i.e.  it is not a case-assigner. Hence,
the subject position of the non-finite IP lacks case. This means that the only way to render
the  sentence grammatical  is  to  insert a  non-overt  NP  in  the  subject position:  (3a)  with
a  non-overt subject  is  grammatical.  If we  say that the  Case-Filter applies  only to overt
| NPs,  | then          | the  grammaticality  |            | of (3a)       | follows.       |             |                           |
| ----- | ------------- | -------------------- | ---------- | ------------- | -------------- | ----------- | ------------------------- |
|       | Consider the  |                      | following  | examples:     |                |             |                           |
|       | (4) a.        | I  know              | [ip  John  | to            | be  the  best  | candidate]. |                           |
|       | b.            | I  don’t             | know       | [cp  whether  | [ip___         |             | to  go  to  the  party]]. |
|       | c.            | *1  don’t            | know       | [cp  whether  | [ip            | John        | to  go  to  the  party]]. |
Know  takes  an  IP  complement.  In  (4b),  the  presence  of whether  indicates  that  we
have  an  infinitival  clause of the  type  CP.  In  this  example,  there  is  no  overt  subject in
the  infinitival  clause,  thus  the  case  filter  does  not  come  into  play  with  respect  to  the
subject  NP  of the  lower  clause.  In  (4c)  know  again  takes  a  clausal  CP  complement
(witness the presence of whether).  In this example the infinitival clause contains an overt
NP  subject  John.  The  sentence  is  ungrammatical  because  it  violates  the  case  filter.
Infinitival  to  is  assumed  to  be  unable to assign  case.  The  potential  case  assigner know
is  separated  from  the  relevant  NP  by  the  maximal  projection  CP,  which  is  a  barrier.
Chapter 3 English Syntax • 91

What English Language Teachers Should Know
| (2)  | Adjectives and  |             | Nouns        |           |                  |     |              |
| ---- | --------------- | ----------- | ------------ | --------- | ---------------- | --- | ------------ |
|      | Nouns  and      | adjectives  |              | are  not  | case  assigners  |     | in  English: |
|      | (5)             | a.  Poirot  | envies       | Miss      | Marple.          |     |              |
|      | b.              | *Poirot     | is  envious  |           | Miss  Marple.    |     |              |
|      | c.              | Poirot      | is  envious  | of Miss   | Marple.          |     |              |
|      | d.              | *Poirot’s   | envy         | Miss      | Marple           |     |              |
|      | e.              | Poirot’s    | envy         | of Miss   | Marple           |     |              |
All  the  examples  in  (5)  contain  a  main  predicate  morphologically  and  semantically
related  to  the verb envy.  In  (5a)  envy,  the verb,  is  used;  in  (5b)  and  (5c)  we  find  the
| related  | adjective  |     | envious;  | in  (5d)  | and  (5e)  | the  | noun  envy. |
| -------- | ---------- | --- | --------- | --------- | ---------- | ---- | ----------- |
In (5a) case assignment is straightforward: Poirot is assigned nominative by the finite
inflection  and Miss  Marple  is  assigned  accusative  by  the  transitive verb  envy.
(5b) is ungrammatical.  If we compare it with the grammatical (5a) the only difference
is  that  we  have  replaced  the  verb  envy by  the  adjective  envious.  Apparently  (5b)  can
be  rescued  by  the  insertion  of the  preposition  of as  seen  in  (5c).
Let  us turn  to  (5d)  and  (5e).  First of all,  we  see  that  these NPs contain a genitive
NP, Poirot's,  in  front of their head N.  Let us assume that there  is an element POSS  in
the specifier position of NPs which is able to assign genitive to the NP in that position.

02 Interpretation of Pronouns
Recall that a personal pronoun can’t have an antecedent in the same clause and that a
reflexive pronoun must have an antecedent in the same clause.
Erma loves her. (Her cannot refer to Erma.)
Erma loves herself (Herself must refer to Erma.)
Now, consider the following sentences. What are the possible antecedents of her and
herself, and how does this evidence complicate our rules of pronoun reference? Think
about the structure of the sentence, in particular the structure of the phrase Erma’s mother.
Erma’s mother loves her.
Erma’s mother loves herself.
Erma can be the antecedent of her, which appears to violate the restriction that pronouns refer to an
antecedent outside of the clause that contains them. But, the difference here seems to be that Erma
is contained inside the subject NP (and therefore for some reason doesn't “count” as the subject NP).
Syntactic accounts of this phenomenon make use of c-command; the NP Erma is more deeply embedded
in the NP - far enough away to allow conference. Erma’s mother is the antecedent of herself here
the whole NP is the antecedent, not just the “inner” part of it.
03 Constituency
Consider the following sentences:
a. Ralph put the note on the door.
b. Ralph found the key to the door.
Provide evidence that (a) and (b) have different structures with respect to their direct
objects and their prepositional phrases.
mumLwm
Passive versions differ in their grammatically if the PP is moved to subject position:
ai: *The note on the door was put by Ralph,
bi: The key to the door was found by Ralph.
94 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr ™
| a?: The note was put on  | the door by Ralph,  |     |     |
| ------------------------ | ------------------- | --- | --- |
b^:  *The key was found to the door by Ralph.
The PP on the door must remain part of the VP in order to yield a grammatical passive. The PP to
the door must remain with the NP the key in order to yield a grammatical  passive.

Evidence 3: clefting
It was the book on that castle that they bought.
*lt was the book that they bought on that castle.
It was the book that they bought at that castle.
*lt was the book at that castle that they bought.

1.  Determiners
| (D Categories  | of determiners |     |     |     |     |

Determiners are words which specify the range of reference of a noun, e.g. by making
it  definite  (the  book),  indefinite  (a  book),  or  by  indicating  quantity  (many  books).

|                    |     | my,  your,                    | his,  her,  etc.    | next,  last,      | other,  etc.       |
|                    |     | Quantifiers:                  |                     | Quantifiers:      |                    |

| (2)  | Sequences  | of determiners |     |     |     |
| ---- | ---------- | -------------- | --- | --- | --- |
Sometimes more than one determiner occurs before a noun. Their ordering is important,
| and  | not  all  sequences  | are             | possible,    | for example, |             |
| ---- | -------------------- | --------------- | ------------ | ------------ | ----------- |
|      | (1) Both             | my  feet  feel  | cold.        |              |             |

|      | (2) All the          | examples        | in  this     | book         | are  clear. |

2.  Articles
| (1) The  | articles  | in  generic  | reference |     |     |
| -------- | --------- | ------------ | --------- | --- | --- |
Generic reference refers to what is general  or typical  for whole class of objects. All
three  types  of article  can  be  used  to  make  a  generic  reference:  the  usually,  and  a/an
always, with singular count nouns; zero with plural count nouns and with noncount nouns.
For  example:
(1)  The  car  became  an  increasing necessity  of life  in  the twentieth  century.
(2) A  car  became  an  increasing  necessity  of life  in  the  twentieth  century.
(3)  Cars  became  an  increasing  necessity  of life  in  the  twentieth  century.
|          | (4)  Velvet  | is  an  excellent  |           | material  | for curtains. |
| -------- | ------------ | ------------------ | --------- | --------- | ------------- |
| (2) The  | articles     | in  specific       | reference |           |               |
The  article  the  marks  a  noun  phrase  as  definite:  that  is,  as  referring  to  something
which can be identified uniquely in the contextual or general knowledge shared by speaker
and  hearer.  Such  shared  knowledge  is  partly  a  knowledge  of the  world  and  partly  a
| knowledge  | of English  | grammar. |     |     |     |
| ---------- | ----------- | -------- | --- | --- | --- |
Where the use of the depends on shared  knowledge of the world, we may speak of
| situational  | reference. |     |     |     |     |
| ------------ | ---------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
102 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr
(1) Do you see the bird sitting on the lower branch?
(2) Oli dear! The stain hasn’t come out of the carpet.
In such cases, the identity of the particular bird, branch, stain, and carpet is obvious
because they arc physically present and visible.
The use of the may be determined by grammatical factors. Grammatical determination
is of two kinds:
(a) Anaphoric reference, where the indicates identity of reference with that established
earlier in the discourse:
(1) Fred bought a radio and a video-recorder, but he returned the radio.
(2) When she tried to open her front door, she couldn’t get the key into the lock.
In (1) the anaphora is direct: but in (2) anaphoric reference may be indirect, requiring
some support from general knowledge. Flere the two definite articles are correctly
interpreted as grammatically anaphoric only because we know that a front door has a
lock, and opening one involves using a key.
(b) Cataphoric reference, where the indicates that the identity of the reference will
be established by what follows:
(1) I am trying to find the book that I wanted to show you.
(2) How did you get the (=that) mud on your coat?
In (1) the is only justified by the addressee knowing that the speaker had planned
to show him or her a book. In (2) there is the presumption that the addressee knows
there is mud on the coat.
3. Genitives
(D The Uses of Genitive
The meanings expressed by the genitive can conveniently be shown through
paraphrase; at the same time, we can compare the analogous use of the ^/•construction.
Chapter 4 English Grammar • 103

soisj-
What English Language Teachers Should Know
(D  Possessive genitive:
| Mrs  Johnson’s  | coat.   | Mrs  Johnson  | owns  this  coat. |
| --------------- | ------- | ------------- | ----------------- |
| The  ship’s     | funnel. | The  ship     | has  a  funnel.   |
© The funnel of the ship.
(2)  Genitive of attribute:
The  victim’s  outstanding  courage. The  victim  was  very  courageous.
© The outstanding courage of the victim.
(3)  Partitive genitive:
The  heart’s  two  ventricles. The  heart  contains  two  ventricles.
© The two ventricles of the heart.
@  Subjective genitive:
| The  parents’  | consent. | The  parents  | consented. |
| -------------- | -------- | ------------- | ---------- |
© The consent of the parents.
(5)  Objective genitive:
| The  prisoner’s  | release. | X  released  | the prisoner. |
| ---------------- | -------- | ------------ | ------------- |
© The release of the prisoner.
(6)  Genitive of origin:
| Mother’s  | letter. |              |                   |
| --------- | ------- | ------------ | ----------------- |
|           |         | The  letter  | is  from  Mother. |
England’s  cheeses. The  cheeses  were  produced  in  England.
© The cheeses of England.
(7)
Descriptive genitive:
Children’s  shoes. The  shoes  are  designed  for  children.
| A doctor’s  | degree. | The  degree  | is  a  doctorate. |
| ----------- | ------- | ------------ | ----------------- |
Sometimes  a  genitive  can  be  ambiguous.  A  phrase  like  Sylvia s  photograph  is
ambiguous  in  three  ways; Sylvia’s  might  be  possessive  genitive  (she  owns  the  photo),
a subjective genitive (she took the photo), or an objective genitive (someone photographed
her). The sentence Liz had heard about Jane’s betrayal has two potential meanings.  If
Jane  betrayed  someone,  the genitive  is a subjective genitive;  if someone  betrayed Jane,
104 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr
the genitive is an objective genitive. The sentence can be “disambiguated”  if the genitive
construction also includes a prepositional phrase that acts as a patient, as in Liz had heard
about Jane’s  betrayal of her friends.  This  is  a  clear example of how  the grammar of
| a  structure  | and  | its  semantics  |     | can  diverge. |     |     |
| ------------- | ---- | --------------- | --- | ------------- | --- | --- |
Grammatically  Jane’s  betrayal  of her friends  is  an  NP  containing  the  noun  head
betrayal which  is  modified  by  the  genitive Jane’s  and the  prepositional  phrase of her
friends. Semantically, however, Jane(’s)  is acting like an agent subject, betrayal is acting
like  a  verb,  and  of her friends  is  a  patient direct  object.  Similarly,  if Jane  is  the  one
being  betrayed,  the  sentence  can  be  “disambiguated”  by  including  an  agent  -  Liz  had
| heard  | about Jane’s     | betrayal by  |     | her  | boss. |     |
| ------ | ---------------- | ------------ | --- | ---- | ----- | --- |
| (2)    | Double  Genitive |              |     |      |       |     |
An  of-construction  can  be  combined  with  an  s-genitive  or  possessive  pronoun  into
| a  ‘double’  | genitive:     |           |         |         |                     |     |
| ------------ | ------------- | --------- | ------- | ------- | ------------------- | --- |
|              | (1)  Shannon  | is  a     | friend  | of my   | wife’s.             |     |
|              | (2) This      | writer’s  | style   | is  no  | favourite  of mine. |     |
The  noun  in  the  genitive  must  be  both  definite  and  personal.  Unlike  the  simple
genitive, the double genitive usually implies that the meaning is not unique, i.e. that ‘my
| wife  | has  several  | friends’.   | Compare:   |       |                 |                |
| ----- | ------------- | ----------- | ---------- | ----- | --------------- | -------------- |
|       | (3) He  is    | Leda’s      | brother.   |       |                 |                |
|       | (suggests     | Leda        | has        | one,  | or  more  than  | one,  brother) |
|       | (4)  He  is   | a  brother  | of Leda’s. |       |                 |                |
|       | (suggests     | Leda        | has        | more  | than  one       | brother)       |

m
Pronouns
| 1.  | The  Interpretation  | of  | Pronouns |     |
| --- | -------------------- | --- | -------- | --- |
Reflexive  pronouns  (the -self pronouns) and  personal  pronouns {he/him,  she/her,  it,
we/us,  etc)  differ  in  an  interesting  way  with  respect  to  the  syntactic  position  of their
linguistic  antecedents.  To  illustrate,  consider the  following  set  of sentence:
|     | John  like   | him.  {Him  cannot       | refer to  | John.)           |
| --- | ------------ | ------------------------ | --------- | ---------------- |
|     | John  likes  | himself.  {Himself must  |           | refer to  John.) |
These data illustrate that although himself requires an antecedent in the same clause,
the opposite is true of him, the antecedent of this pronoun cannot be in the same clause.
| Consider  | the             | following  examples: |             |         |
| --------- | --------------- | -------------------- | ----------- | ------- |
|           | Elmer thinks    | that  Irma           | must  know  | him.    |
|           | * Elmer thinks  | that  Irma           | must  know  | himself |
In the first sentence, him occurs in a subordinate clause (the underlined section). Him
can  refer to Elmer because  in  this case Elmer is outside of the clause  containing him.
The second  sentence shows that the reflexive pronoun himself on  the other hand, can’t
take Elmer as an antecedent, because Elmer is outside the (underlined) clause containing
the reflexive. This evidence again suggests not only that we are very much aware of clause
boundaries  in  general  but  also  that  we  can  easily  (unconsciously,  at  least)  identify
subordinate clauses.  We can informally state the rules of pronoun reference, which depend
| on  | our  understanding  | of clause  | boundaries,  | as  follows: |
| --- | ------------------- | ---------- | ------------ | ------------ |
Principle A:
A  reflexive  pronoun  must  have  an  antecedent inside  of the  clause  that  contains  it.
Principle B:
A  personal  pronoun  must  have  an  antecedent outside of the  clause that  contains  it.
106 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.krV
| 2.  Reflexive  | Pronouns |     |
| -------------- | -------- | --- |
The basic  reflexive  pronoun  takes  the  function of a  noun phrase  in  the  structure of
the clause or phrase:  it may be an object, a complement, or a prepositional complement.
Since it corefers to the subject, however, the reflexive pronoun cannot itself be a subject.
In this, it shows that it belongs to ‘object territory’, and has affinities with the objective
personal  pronoun.  But  it  contrasts  with  the  objective  pronoun  in  meaning  in  examples
| such  as  | this:             |                  |
| --------- | ----------------- | ---------------- |
| (1) He    | saw  himself/him  | in  the  mirror. |
In (1), himself is necessarily preferential to the subject he, while him necessarily refers
to  some  other  person.  We  have  to  use  the  reflexive  rather than  the  ordinary  objective
pronoun. The reflexive pronoun must agree with the subject in terms of gender, number,
and  person:
| She  | saw  herself/*himself in  | the  mirror. |
| ---- | ------------------------- | ------------ |
For  an  object,  complement,  or  prepositional  object  which  is  preferential  with  the
| subject,  | the  reflexive  pronoun  | is  obligatory. |
| --------- | ------------------------ | --------------- |
The  basic  reflexive  pronoun  always  prefers  to  the  subject  of its  own  clause,  even
though  that  subject  may  be  ‘understood’.  For example,  the  implied  subject  of the  -ing
participle  clause  in  (2)  is  Vincent,  and  himself is  therefore  the  appropriate  reflexive
pronoun:
(2)  Freeing  himself with  a  sharp  knife,  Vincent  lurched  towards  the  door.
In  (3),  it  is John who  is  understood  to  be  the  subject  of the  infinitive  clause,  and
the appropriate reflexive is therefore himself even though the subject of the main clause
is  she:
| (3)  | She  asked  John  | to  invite himself/*herself |
| ---- | ----------------- | --------------------------- |

|     | connection  | with  | the  | preceding  | verb: |     |     |     |

(ii) When a reflexive pronoun (particularly a  1st person  pronoun) is coordinated

This  is  a  great tribute to  the Scout Movement,  and to you/yourself as  its

| 3.  | Reflexive  |     | and  | Reciprocal  |     | Pronouns |     |     |
| --- | ---------- | --- | ---- | ----------- | --- | -------- | --- | --- |
Reflexive and reciprocal  pronouns replace NP objects that have the same referent as
the  subject  of the  sentence.  The  coreference  must  be  within  the  clause;  thus  we  have
| a  contrast  | between        |     |         |       |     |                       |             |     |
| ------------ | -------------- | --- | ------- | ----- | --- | --------------------- | ----------- | --- |
|              | (1)  Penelope  |     | begged  | Jane  | to  | look  after her.      | (=Penelope) |     |
|              | Penelope       |     | begged  | Jane  | to  | look  after herself.  | (=Jane)     |     |
(2) The  men/,  will  shoot  the  arrows/ at each  otherj.  (=the  arrows)
|     | The          | men/     | will      | shoot the       |      | arrows/  at each  | otheri.  (=the  | men) |
| --- | ------------ | -------- | --------- | --------------- | ---- | ----------------- | --------------- | ---- |
|     | (3) John     | and      | Mary      | despise         |      | themselves.       |                 |      |
|     | (=           | John     | despises  | John,           | and  | Mary  despises    | Mary.)          |      |
|     | John         | and      | Mary      | despise         |      | each  other.      |                 |      |
|     | (=           | John     | despises  | Mary            | and  | Mary  despises    | John.)          |      |
| U.  | Relative     | Pronoun  |           | Clauses         |      |                   |                 |      |
| (D  | Restrictive  | versus   |           | Nonrestrictive  |      | Relative          | Clauses         |      |
A restrictive  relative  clause  is  one  that  serves  to  restrict the  reference  of the  noun
phrase modified.  In (1),  the  restrictive relative clause who lives in Canada restricts my
sister by specifying the sister in Canada. The sentence implies that the speaker has more
than  one  sister,  but only  one sister  in  Canada  is  a  biologist.  It  could  be  an  answer to
the question  Which of your sisters is a biologist? The information added by the relative
| clause  | identifies  |         | the  sister. 1 |        |     |                |            |     |
| ------- | ----------- | ------- | -------------- | ------ | --- | -------------- | ---------- | --- |
|         | (1)  My     | sister  | who            | lives  | in  | Canada  is  a  | biologist. |     |
110 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr p
Sentence (2) contains a nonrestrictive relative clause, indicated as such by the commas
around it. A nonrestrictive relative clause adds information about the noun modified. The
noun’s reference  is already clear, the clause does not restrict it.  Thus,  in (2) the relative
clause is just an added comment to the main clause content my sister is a biologist.  The
relative  clause  in  essence  says  “Oh,  by  the  way,  she  lives  in  Canada.”  There  is  no
| implication  | that  the         | speaker  |        | has  other   | sisters. |                   |
| ------------ | ----------------- | -------- | ------ | ------------ | -------- | ----------------- |
|              | (2)  My  sister,  | who      | lives  | in  Canada,  |          | is  a  biologist. |
(2)  Form Criteria Distinguishing Nonrestrictive and Restrictive Relative Clauses
Several  form  criteria  distinguish  nonrestrictive  relative  clauses  from  their restrictive
counterparts.
©  Punctuation
Nonrestrictive  relative clauses have commas around them,  as  in (3a).  Restrictive
relative  clauses  must  not  be  separated  by  commas,  as  shown  in  (3b).
|     | (3) a. My  sister,  |     | who  | lives  in Canada, is  |     | a  biologist,     |
| --- | ------------------- | --- | ---- | --------------------- | --- | ----------------- |
|     | b. My  sister       |     | who  | lives  in Canada      |     | is  a  biologist. |
©  Modification of proper nouns
Nonrestrictive  relative  clauses  can  modify  proper  nouns,  as  in  (4a);  restrictive
|     | relatives,  as  | in  | (4b),  | cannot. |     |     |
| --- | --------------- | --- | ------ | ------- | --- | --- |
(4) a. John,  who  is  a  linguist, was not impressed  by  Professor Fish’s arguments,
b. *John  who  is  a  linguist was  not impressed  by  Professor Fish’s arguments.
|     | ©  Modification of any, every, no,  |     |     |     | etc. |     |
| --- | ----------------------------------- | --- | --- | --- | ---- | --- |
Nonrestrictive  relative  clauses  may  not  modify  any,  every,  or  no  +  noun  or
indefinite  pronouns  such  as  anyone,  everyone,  or  no  one,  as  shown  by  (5a);
|     | restrictive  | relatives  | may,  | as  shown  | in  | (5b). |
| --- | ------------ | ---------- | ----- | ---------- | --- | ----- |
(5) a.  *Any  man,  who  goes  back  on  his  word,  is  no  friend  of mine,
b.  Any  man  who  goes  back  on  his  word  is  no  friend  of mine.

What English Language Teachers Should Know
| @   | That  as relative pronoun |     |     |     |     |
| --- | ------------------------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
Nonrestrictive relative clauses may not be introduced by that,  as shown  by (6a);
|     | restrictive  relatives  | may,  | as  in  | (6b). |     |
| --- | ----------------------- | ----- | ------- | ----- | --- |
(6) a.  *The  plan,  that we  discussed  yesterday,  will  be  adopted,
|     | b.  The  plan  | that  we  | discussed  | yesterday  will  | be  adopted. |
| --- | -------------- | --------- | ---------- | ---------------- | ------------ |
(5)  Stacking
Nonrestrictive relative clauses cannot be stacked. Stacking results in ungrammatical
sentences  like  (7a).  Restrictive  relatives  can  be  stacked,  as  in  (7b).
(7) a.  *They gave the job to  Rob, who  is very qualified, who starts next month,
b.  I  really like that car that you have that your wife is always zipping around
|     | town  in. |     |     |     |     |
| --- | --------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
(6)  Sentence modification
Nonrestrictive relative clauses may modify an entire sentence, that is, a preceding
independent  clauses,  as  in  (8a).  Restrictive  relatives  like  (8b)  may  only  modify
|     | noun  phrases. |     |     |     |     |
| --- | -------------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
(8) a.  Professor  Fish  gave  everyone  an  A,  which  was just fine with  Alice,
b.  *Professor  Fish  gave  everyone  an  A  which  was just  fine  with  Alice.

Adverbs
j
| 1.  Stative  | and  | Dynamic  | Adjectives |     |     |
| ------------ | ---- | -------- | ---------- | --- | --- |
Adjectives  are  characteristically  stative.  Many  adjectives,  however,  can  be  seen  as
dynamic. In particular, most adjectives that are susceptible to subjective measurement are
capable of being dynamic. Stative and dynamic adjectives differ syntactically in a number
of ways. For example, a stative adjective such as tall cannot be used with the progressive
| aspect  | or  with  the  | imperative: |     |     |     |
| ------- | -------------- | ----------- | --- | --- | --- |
112 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr
*He’s being tall. *Be tall.
On the other hand, we can use careful as a dynamic adjective:
He’s being careful. Be careful.
A general semantic feature of dynamic adjectives seems to be that they denote qualities
that are thought to be subject to control by the possessor and hence can be restricted
temporally.
Adjectives that can be used dynamically include:
brave, calm, cheerful, conceited, cruel, foolish, friendly, funny, good, greedy,
helpful, jealous, naughty, noisy, tidy, etc.
2. Unmarked and Marked Adjectives
When we want to state the age of a person, we will normally use the expression x
years old, for example:
Mr Jespersen is 75 years old.
We will not say:
*Mr Jespersen is 75 years young.
However, the choice of adjective here has nothing to do with the number of years,
and we would use the same expression for somebody very young, for example:
His granddaughter is two years old/*young.
Similarly:
The water is two meters deep/*shallow.
Susan is only five feet tall/*short.
‘Measure’ adjectives like old, deep, and tall cover a scale of measurement. Such
adjectives have two terms for the opposite ranges of the scale (old/young, deep/shallow,
tall/short), but use the upper range as the ‘unmarked’ term in measure expressions (old,
Chapter U English Grammar • 113

What English Language Teachers Should Know
deep,  tall).  The  ‘measure’  adjeelives  which  are  used  in  this  way  include  the  following
| set  | with   | the  marked  | term  | give  | in  parenthesis: |              |               |
| ---- | ------ | ------------ | ----- | ----- | ---------------- | ------------ | ------------- |
|      | big    | (small)      |       | deep  | (shallow)        | fast  (slow) | hard  (soft)  |
|      | high   | (low)        |       | long  | (short)          | old  (young) | tall  (short) |
|      | thick  | (thin)       |       | wide  | (narrow)         |              |               |
This set of ‘measure’adjectives is used in the same way in /tow-questions and, again,
the  use of the  unmarked term does not assume that only  the upper range  is applicable:
|     | A:  | How      | old  is    | your daughter?  | [‘What     | is  her age?’]    |     |
| --- | --- | -------- | ---------- | --------------- | ---------- | ----------------- | --- |
|     | B:  | She  is  | ten years  | old.            | [‘Her age  | is  ten  years.’] |     |
There are also adjectives outside the set listed above which are used in //tm'-questions,
in  the  same  way  as  the  ‘measure’  adjectives.  Thus,  if we  want  to  know  the  size  of
something, we would normally use big or large,  rather than small or little, for example:
|     | How  | big  | is  your  | computer? |     |     |     |
| --- | ---- | ---- | --------- | --------- | --- | --- | --- |
Similarly, we use bright rather than dim, strong rather than weak,  heavy rather than
light,  etc:
|     | How strong  |           | is  the  | engine  | of this         | car? |     |
| --- | ----------- | --------- | -------- | ------- | --------------- | ---- | --- |
|     | How         | accurate  | is       | your    | digital  watch? |      |     |
3.  Distinguishing  Adjectival  Participles  from Verbal  Participles
Often  the  difference  between  the  adjective  and  the  participle  is  not  clear-cut.  The
verbal  force of the participle is explicit for the -ing form when a direct object is present.
Hence,  the  following  -ing  forms  are  participles  that  constitute  a  verb  phrase  with  the
| preceding  |                | auxiliary:       |                 |           |                |     |     |
| ---------- | -------------- | ---------------- | --------------- | --------- | -------------- | --- | --- |
|            | (1) Her views  |                  | were            | alarming  | her audience.  |     |     |
|            | (2) You        | are frightening  |                 |           | the  children. |     |     |
|            | (3) They       |                  | are  insulting  |           | us.            |     |     |
1U
 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr
Similarly, the verbal force is explicit for the -ed form when a Ay-agent phrase with
a personal agent is present, indicating the correspondence to the active form of the
sentence:
(1) The man was offended by the policeman.
(2) He is appreciated by his students.
(3) She was misunderstood by her parents.
For both participle forms, premodification by the intensifier very is an explicit
indication that the forms have achieved adjective status:
(1) Her views were very alarming.
(2) You are very frightening.
(3) The man was very offended.
We might therefore expect that the presence of very together with an explicit indicator
of verbal force would produce an unacceptable sentence.
*His views were very alarming his audience.
Adjectival Use of -ing and -ed Participles
U.
There is a distinction in English between pairs of adjectives such as boring/ bored,
exciting/ excited, and shocking shocked. These forms are normally used as in examples
(la, b, and c), but many learners produce versions of the type shown in (Id and e). 1
(1) a. The lesson was interesting.
b. The teacher was amusing.
c. The students were interested and amused.
d. 1 didn’t like the lesson. I was boring.
e. I am more interesting in English grammar.
Chapter U English Grammar • 115

What English Language Teachers Should Know
In  examples  such  as  (Id  and  e),  learners  may  use  the  -ing  form  of the  adjective
(boring, interesting) when they actually mean the -ed form (bored, interested).  But why,
they  may  ask,  can’t they  say,  I am boring today?  The  answer  is,  of course, that they
certainly can say that (it’s grammatically correct), but is it what they mean? The meaning
| distinction  | is  not  | too  difficult  | to  explain. |     |     |
| ------------ | -------- | --------------- | ------------ | --- | --- |
These adjectives are derived from verbs that express emotions or feelings.  When we
talk about an  emotion,  we  can  focus  on  the  source  (i.e.  who  or what  causes  it)  or on
the experiencer (i.e. who or what is affected by it). When you are talking about the source,
you  use the -ing  form.  If a  book (or a  lesson or a  person)  causes the emotion, then  it
is boring,  interesting,  or exciting.  When you are talking about the experiencer, you use
the -ed form.  If people experience the emotion, then they are bored, interested, or excited.
| The  cause  | is  boring,  | the  experiencer  | is  bored. |     |     |
| ----------- | ------------ | ----------------- | ---------- | --- | --- |
We can take these observations and summarize them, as shown  in  Box  1.1.  We can
also  use this opportunity to  include  a  list (as a teaching resource) of several  adjectives
| that  have  | this  meaning  | distinction. |     |     |     |
| ----------- | -------------- | ------------ | --- | --- | --- |
Summary B©k 1.1 Adjectives like boring and bored
| Source or cause is -ing |              |           |     | Experiencer or affected is -ed |                   |
| ----------------------- | ------------ | --------- | --- | ------------------------------ | ----------------- |
| Things                  | or  people   | are:      |     | People                         | (mostly)  are     |
| amazing,                | amusing,     | annoying, |     | amazed,                        | amused,  annoyed, |
| astonishing,            | bewildering, |           |     | astonished,                    | bewildered,       |
boring,  confusing,  depressing, bored,  confused,  depressed,
| disappointing,           |             | disgusting, |     | disappointed,          | disgusted, |
| ------------------------ | ----------- | ----------- | --- | ---------------------- | ---------- |
| embarrassing,            |             | exciting,   |     | embarrassed,           | excited    |
| exhausting, fascinating, |             |             |     | exhausted, fascinated, |            |
| frightening,             | horrifying, |             |     | frightened,            | horrified, |
|                          |             |             |     | interested,            | intrigued, |
| interesting,             | intriguing, |             |     |                        |            |
irritating, puzzling,  satisfying, irritated,  puzzled,  satisfied,
| shocking,    | surprising, |          |     | shocked,    | surprised,      |
