•flapping
N  and  /d/  become  [r]  when  they  occur  between  two  vowels,  the  first  of which  is
stressed  and  the  second  of which  is  unstressed,  (e.g.,  rider  [rairor]).
• flouting
The  intentional  violation  of a conversational  maxim  for the  purpose of conveying an
unstated  proposition  (e.g., I  could eat  a  horse —>  ‘I  am  hungry’).
•foot
Each  phonological  foot  starts  with  a  stressed  syllable,  and  continues  up  to,  but  not
| including,  | the  next  stressed  | syllable. |     |     |
| ----------- | -------------------- | --------- | --- | --- |
• free  morpheme
| One  that  | can  stand  alone  | as  a  word  | (e.g.,  man). |     |
| ---------- | ------------------ | ------------ | ------------- | --- |
•free  variation
Two items that can occur in the same environment without causing a change in meaning
| (e.g.,  [J]  | and  [z]  are  | in  free  variation  | in garage). |     |
| ------------ | -------------- | -------------------- | ----------- | --- |
• fricative
A consonant produced  with a  partial  obstruction of the  airstream  in  which the air is
pushed  through  a  narrow  constriction,  resulting  in  a  friction  noise  (e.g.,  /f,  s,  e/).

Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • 275

What English Language Teachers Should Know
• heavy  NP shift
Movement  to  the  right  of a  noun  phrase  that  is  heavy  by  virtue  of containing  a
postmodifying  prepositional  phrase or clause,  (e.g.,  in the  following  sentence the NP
three hibles that were hound in leather has been moved across the PP to Rome under
HNPS:  1 sent _  to  Rome  [NP three  hibles  that  were  bound in  leather],
• hedges
Words or phrases used to indicate that we are not really sure that what we are saying
is sufficiently correct or complete,  (e.g., as far as / know,  to cut a long story short,
I guess).
| • hierarchical  | structure |     |     |
| --------------- | --------- | --- | --- |
The  grouping  of words  within  phrases  (e.g.,  [old [English  teacher]]).
• homonymy
One word-form with two unrelated meanings (e.g., key ‘device for disengaging a lock'
| and  ‘small  | island'). |     |     |
| ------------ | --------- | --- | --- |
• hyponymy
The  sense  of one  word  (the  superordinate)  being  entirely  contained  in  the  sense  of
another word  (the  hyponym)  (e.g.,  the  sense  of talk  is  entirely  contained  within  the
sense  of mumble).

| • literal  locutionary  |        | act |

sarcastic  or exaggerated).  Any  other  locutionary  act  is  nonliteral.

The  production  of the  sounds  and  words  that  make  up  an  utterance.

| • maximal           | onset principle |     |
Where there  is a choice, always assign as many consonants as possible to  the onset,
| and  as  few  | as  possible  | to  the  coda. |
| ------------- | ------------- | -------------- |
• maxim  of manner
This  maxim  states  that  each  participant’s  contribution  should  be  expressed  in  a
reasonably  clear  fashion;  that  is,  it  should  not  be  vague,  ambiguous,  or excessively
wordy. •
| • maxim  | of quality |     |
| -------- | ---------- | --- |
This maxim state that each participant’s contribution should be truthful and  based  on
| sufficient  | evidence. |     |

0 nasal

| • performative  | verb |     |     |
| --------------- | ---- | --- | --- |
A verb that actually names the illocutionary act it is used to perform (e.g., state,  order,
| question,        | etc.).    |                    |                  |
| ---------------- | --------- | ------------------ | ---------------- |
| • perloctionary  | act       |                    |                  |
| The  effect      | achieved  | by  an  utterance  | on  the  hearer. |
• phoneme
The smallest unit of sound  in  a  language which  can  distinguish  two  words,  (e.g.,  in
English, the words pan and  ban differ only  in  their initial  sound.  Therefore, /p/ and
| /b/  are        | phonemes  of English). |     |     |
| --------------- | ---------------------- | --- | --- |
| • phonological  | rules                  |     |     |
Generalizations  describing  the  pronunciation  of segments  according  to  their  position
| (e.g.,  vowels  | are  lengthened  | before  | voiced  consonants). |
| --------------- | ---------------- | ------- | -------------------- |
| • phonotactic   | constraints      |         |                      |
Restrictions  on  the  permissible  sequences  of segments  in  a  language  (e.g.,  if three
consonants begin a word  in English, the first one must be /s/ and  the third one must
| be  a  liquid  | or  a  glide). |     |     |
| -------------- | -------------- | --- | --- |
| • phrasal      | verb           |     |     |
A complex intransitive or transitive verb that consists of two parts: a verb and a particle,
(e.g.,  He  looked up  the  word/He  looked the  word up.  She gave  up.)
• phrase
A string of words that behaves as a constituent and has a head as its principal element. •
| • phrase  | marker |     |     |
| --------- | ------ | --- | --- |
A  graphic  representation  of the  structure  of a  (subpart  of a)  sentence,  alternatively
| known  | as  a  tree  or tree diagram. |     |     |
| ------ | ----------------------------- | --- | --- |
282 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

www.amoriteaching.co.kr^
° pitch
The perceptual correlate of the frequency of a sound. The higher the frequency of vocal
cord vibration, the higher the pitch.
• polysemy
One word-form with two related meanings (e.g., bright means either ‘intelligent or
‘shining’).
• predicate
A semantic label which denotes an element that requires the specification of the
participants (arguments) in the proposition expressed, (e.g., in the sentence We like
parties the predicate is the verb like which requires a Subject argument and a Direct
Object argument).
• predicate)r
A functional label applied to the verb in a sentence or clause.
• prepositional verb
A verb that takes a prepositional phrase as its Complement, (e.g., rely on NP, look
at NP)
• presupposition
A proposition expressed in a sentence that is assumed to be true in order to judge
the truth of another sentence (e.g., Hart passed the bar exam presupposes Hart took
the bar exam).
• presupposition trigger
A word or structure that presupposes the truth of a proposition or the speaker’s attitude
about it (e.g., Why are you in a bad mood? presupposes You are in a bad mood). •
• pro-form substitution
A test for determining whether a structural unit is a head, bar, or phrasal category (e.g.,
one typically substitutes for N-bar categories, as in I studied more for the last French
quiz than for the first one).

| • pseudocleft        | sentence |           |        |                 |          |       |

• raising
A process which involves the displacement of a noun phrase from the Subject position
of a subordinate nonfinite clause typically to the main clause Subject position. A raising
verb  is  a  verb  that  can  appear in  a  raising construction,  (e.g.,  Martova seems [_ to
be happy]).
| • reduced  | vowel |     |     |     |     |     |
| ---------- | ----- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
A vowel that is pronounced with a centralized schwa ([o]) quality (e.g. the first vowel
| of photography  |            | and  the  | second  | vowel  of photograph). |     |     |
| --------------- | ---------- | --------- | ------- | ---------------------- | --- | --- |

A  linguistic  expression  which  denotes  a  person  or entiry  in  the  real  world,  (e.g.,  in

| with  the  | name Nick). |     |     |     |     |     |
| ---------- | ----------- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
• reverses
The characteristic  reverse relation  is  between  terms describing movement, where one
term  describes  movement  in  one  direction, — and  the  other the same movement in
the  opposite  direction,  ,  (e.g.,  inflate/deflate,  expand/contract,  full/empty  or
knit/unravel).
0 rhyme
The  part  of a  syllable  that  follows  the  onset  (e.g.  /int/  in print).
| • right  node  | raising |     |     |     |     |     |
| -------------- | ------- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
A displacement  process  exemplified  by  the  following  sentence:
| Pete  | bought _,  | but  | Hans  read  | _,  today’s  | newspaper. |     |
| ----- | ---------- | ---- | ----------- | ------------ | ---------- | --- |
Here, the noun phrase today’s newspaper,  which acts as the Direct Object of both buy
and  read,  has  been  moved  to  the  right  and  is  raised  from  a  right node  position.

0 root
The morpheme in a word that carries the major component of the word’s meaning and
belongs  to  a  lexical  category,  (e.g.,  collect  in  the  word  collections).
• segment
A unit of phonology corresponding roughly to “speech sound”; allophones, phonemes,
and morphophonemes are all segments. We interpret the physical speech signal  in terms
of segments.

What English Language Teachers Should Know
• sonorant
| Any  member  | of the  class        | of nasals,  | liquids,  and  | glides. |

| • sonority   | sequencing principle |             |                |         |
Principles  underlying  syllable  structure:  onsets  must  rise  in  sonority,  codas  fall  in
| sonority,  | and  nuclei  contain  | the  sonority  | peak  | of the  syllable. |

• source
A thematic  role  that  denotes  the  location  or  entity  from  which  something  moves.
• specialization
Semantic change in which the scope of a word narrows (e.g., deer used to means "any
wild animal’).
• specifier
In  X-bar  syntax,  a  modifier that always  occurs  first in  a  phrase,  before an  adjunct,
complement, or head (e.g., the determiner that in the NP that tall math student). The
term  specifier  designates  a  position  (like  subject,  direct  object,  etc.),  not  a  class  of
| words  (like  | adjective,  noun,  | etc.). |     |     |
| ------------- | ------------------ | ------ | --- | --- |
| • speech      | act                |        |     |     |
A locutionary act (i.e., the act of saying something) plus an illocutionary act (i.e., the
| act  of doing  | something). • |     |     |     |
| -------------- | ------------- | --- | --- | --- |
• stative  verb
A verb that expresses a state or condition rather than an action, (e.g., have, know,  like,
need,  own).
286 • What English Language Teachers Should Know

lljjp
www.amoriteaching.co.kr^sr
• stem
The base to which an affix is attached to create a more complex form that may be
another stem or a word, (e.g., modification is the stem for -s in the word modifications).
° stop
A consonant sound that is produced with a complete closure in some part of the vocal
tract, followed by an abrupt release (e.g. /p, t, k, b, d, g/).
• structural ambiguity
The condition whereby an expression can be assigned more than one syntactic structure
(e.g., old men and women = old [men and women] or [old men] and women).
• subcategorization
The requirement of a predicate to take a category (or categories) of a particular type
as its Complement, (e.g., the verb devour subcategories a noun phrase, because we
cannot leave out the NP in a sentence that contains this verb: *The kids devoured.)
The syntactic ‘needs’ of predicates are specified in subcategorisation frames.
• subcategorization frame
A graphic way of representing the subcategorisation requirements of a particular
predicate, (e.g., the subcategorisation frame for the verb devour is as follows:)
devour (verb)
[_, NP]
• subject dependent inversion
The elements inverted are the subject and another dependent of the verb. The latter is
usually a complement - most commonly a locative or an adjectival predicative
complement, (e.g., A bowl of fruit was on her desk —> On her desk was a bowl of fruit.) •
• substitution
The replacement of a word or string of words by a proform.

A sentence that has an ease/difficulty adjective (e.g., tough, fan, simple,  easy, pleasant,

| (e.g.,  Films,  | I   | enjoy _.) |     |     |     |     |     |

• v-har  deletion
The  deletion  of a  V-bar  constituent,  as  in  the  following  example:  Henry will travel
to South America,  but Anna wont _, where the V-bar travel to South America has been
deleted.•
• velar
An  articulation  involving  the  back  of the  tongue  and  the  velum  (e.g.  /k,  g,  ij/).
Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • 289

What English Language Teachers Should Know
• velarization
Raising the back of the tongue toward the velum, as in the dark /l/ (e.g. full).
• verb complementation
This term refers to the kinds of Complement(s) that a verb can take.
• vowel reduction
A procee that reduces a full vowel, typically unstressed, to a schwa, (e.g., photograph
[fotograef] vs. photography [fotagrofr]).
• vowel epenthesis
Insertion of a vowel (typically [a]) to break up a series of consonants (e.g., pastty
[pestri] —* [pestari]).
• weak/reduced form
The common, unstressed form of a function word (e.g. [dat] for that).
• wh-movement
The displacement of a phrase that contains a word that begins with the letters ‘wh’.
For example, in Which film did you see? the wh-phrase which film has been moved
from a position immediately following the verb to the beginning of the sentence.)
• vp-preposing
The movement of a verb phrase to a sentence-initial or clause-initial position. For
example:
He said that he would go to Rio, and go to Rio he did _.
Here by VP go to Rio has been fronted under VP-Preposing.
• X-bar syntax
A theory of constituent structure that recognizes a structural unit intermediate in size
between a phrasal and a head category (e.g., N-bar or N' is an intermediate category
between NP and N).
